<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Family</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Group of related people</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the group of related people. For the taxonomic rank, see <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">Family (biology)</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Family_(disambiguation)" title="Family (disambiguation)">Family (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Family life" redirects here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Family_Life_(disambiguation)" title="Family Life (disambiguation)">Family Life</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Family member" redirects here. For the song, see <a href="/wiki/Punk_(Chai_album)" title="Punk (Chai album)">Punk (Chai album)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Blood harmony" redirects here. For the EP, see <a href="/wiki/Blood_Harmony" title="Blood Harmony">Blood Harmony</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sauk_Indian_family_by_Frank_Rinehart_1899.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4482" data-file-width="3631" decoding="async" height="272" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Sauk_Indian_family_by_Frank_Rinehart_1899.jpg/220px-Sauk_Indian_family_by_Frank_Rinehart_1899.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Sauk_Indian_family_by_Frank_Rinehart_1899.jpg/330px-Sauk_Indian_family_by_Frank_Rinehart_1899.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Sauk_Indian_family_by_Frank_Rinehart_1899.jpg/440px-Sauk_Indian_family_by_Frank_Rinehart_1899.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sauk_Indian_family_by_Frank_Rinehart_1899.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sauk_people" title="Sauk people">Sauk</a> family photographed by <a href="/wiki/Frank_Rinehart" title="Frank Rinehart">Frank Rinehart</a> in 1899</div></div></div>
<p><b>Family</b> (from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">familia</i>) is a <a href="/wiki/Social_group" title="Social group">group</a> of people related either by <a href="/wiki/Consanguinity" title="Consanguinity">consanguinity</a> (by recognized birth) or <a href="/wiki/Affinity_(law)" title="Affinity (law)">affinity</a> (by marriage or other relationship). The purpose of the family is to maintain the well-being of its members and of society. Ideally, families offer predictability, structure, and safety as members mature and learn to participate in the community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> Historically, most human societies use family as the primary locus of <a href="/wiki/Attachment_theory" title="Attachment theory">attachment</a>, nurturance, and <a href="/wiki/Socialization" title="Socialization">socialization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Anthropologist" title="Anthropologist">Anthropologists</a> classify most family organizations as <a href="/wiki/Matrifocal_family" title="Matrifocal family">matrifocal</a> (a mother and her children), patrifocal (a father and his children), <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/conjugal" title="wikt:conjugal">conjugal</a> (a wife, her husband, and children, also called the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_family" title="Nuclear family">nuclear family</a>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avuncular" title="Avuncular">avuncular</a> (a man, his sister, and her children), or <a href="/wiki/Extended_family" title="Extended family">extended</a> (in addition to parents and children, may include grandparents, aunts, uncles, or cousins).
</p><p>The field of <a href="/wiki/Genealogy" title="Genealogy">genealogy</a> aims to trace family lineages through history. The family is also an important economic unit studied in <a href="/wiki/Family_economics" title="Family economics">family economics</a>. The word "families" can be used metaphorically to create more inclusive categories such as <a href="/wiki/Community" title="Community">community</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nation" title="Nation">nationhood</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_village_(term)" title="Global village (term)">global village</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Social">Social</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Social">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galla_Placidia_(rechts)_und_ihre_Kinder.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1438" data-file-width="1407" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Galla_Placidia_%28rechts%29_und_ihre_Kinder.jpg/220px-Galla_Placidia_%28rechts%29_und_ihre_Kinder.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Galla_Placidia_%28rechts%29_und_ihre_Kinder.jpg/330px-Galla_Placidia_%28rechts%29_und_ihre_Kinder.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Galla_Placidia_%28rechts%29_und_ihre_Kinder.jpg/440px-Galla_Placidia_%28rechts%29_und_ihre_Kinder.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galla_Placidia_(rechts)_und_ihre_Kinder.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Detail of a <a href="/wiki/Gold_glass" title="Gold glass">gold glass</a> medallion with a portrait of a family, from <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a> (<a href="/wiki/Roman_Egypt" title="Roman Egypt">Roman Egypt</a>), 3rd–4th century (<a href="/wiki/Brescia" title="Brescia">Brescia</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Museo_di_Santa_Giulia" title="Museo di Santa Giulia">Museo di Santa Giulia</a>) <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></div></div></div><p>One of the primary functions of the family involves providing a framework for the production and reproduction of persons <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biologically" title="Biologically">biologically</a> and socially. This can occur through the sharing of material substances (such as food); the giving and receiving of care and nurture (<a href="/wiki/Nurture_kinship" title="Nurture kinship">nurture kinship</a>); jural rights and obligations; and moral and sentimental ties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schneider_p._182_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schneider_p._182-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Thus, one's experience of one's family shifts over time. From the perspective of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Children" title="Children">children</a>, the family is a "family of orientation": the family serves to locate children socially and plays a major role in their <a href="/wiki/Enculturation" title="Enculturation">enculturation</a> and socialization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> From the point of view of the parent(s), the family is a "family of procreation", the goal of which is to produce, enculturate and socialize children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> However, producing children is not the only function of the family; in societies with a sexual division of labor, <a href="/wiki/Marriage" title="Marriage">marriage</a>, and the resulting relationship between two people, it is necessary for the formation of an economically productive <a href="/wiki/Household" title="Household">household</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>C. C. Harris notes that the western conception of family is ambiguous and confused with the <a href="/wiki/Household" title="Household">household</a>, as revealed in the different contexts in which the word is used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Olivia_Harris" title="Olivia Harris">Olivia Harris</a> states this confusion is not accidental, but indicative of the familial ideology of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Capitalist" title="Capitalist">capitalist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">western</a> countries that pass social legislation that insists members of a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_family" title="Nuclear family">nuclear family</a> should live together, and that those not so related should not live together; despite the ideological and legal pressures, a large percentage of families do not conform to the ideal <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_family" title="Nuclear family">nuclear family</a> type.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Size">Size</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Size">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Fertility_factor_(demography)" title="Fertility factor (demography)">Fertility factor (demography)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mennonite_siblings_Montana_1937.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="386" data-file-width="529" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Mennonite_siblings_Montana_1937.jpg/220px-Mennonite_siblings_Montana_1937.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Mennonite_siblings_Montana_1937.jpg/330px-Mennonite_siblings_Montana_1937.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Mennonite_siblings_Montana_1937.jpg/440px-Mennonite_siblings_Montana_1937.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mennonite_siblings_Montana_1937.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mennonite" title="Mennonite">Mennonite</a> siblings, Montana, United States, 1937</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Total_fertility_rate" title="Total fertility rate">total fertility rate</a> of women varies from country to country, from a high of 6.76 children born/woman in <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">Niger</a> to a low of 0.81 in <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a> (as of 2015).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cia.gov_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-cia.gov-14">[14]</a></sup> Fertility is low in most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_European" title="Eastern European">Eastern European</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_European" title="Southern European">Southern European</a> countries, and high in most <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan African</a> countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cia.gov_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-cia.gov-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>In some cultures, the mother's preference of family size influences that of the children through early adulthood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AxinnClarkberg1994_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-AxinnClarkberg1994-15">[15]</a></sup> A parent's number of children strongly correlates with the number of children that their children will eventually have.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murphy2013_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Murphy2013-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types"><span class="anchor" id="Family_types"></span>Types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-A0808-0008-001,_Berlin,_Passanten.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="790" data-file-width="582" decoding="async" height="299" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-A0808-0008-001%2C_Berlin%2C_Passanten.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-A0808-0008-001%2C_Berlin%2C_Passanten.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-A0808-0008-001%2C_Berlin%2C_Passanten.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-A0808-0008-001%2C_Berlin%2C_Passanten.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-A0808-0008-001%2C_Berlin%2C_Passanten.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-A0808-0008-001%2C_Berlin%2C_Passanten.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-A0808-0008-001,_Berlin,_Passanten.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A German mother with her children in the 1960s</div></div></div>
<p>Although early western <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cultural_anthropologist" title="Cultural anthropologist">cultural anthropologists</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sociologist" title="Sociologist">sociologists</a> considered family and <a href="/wiki/Kinship" title="Kinship">kinship</a> to be universally associated with relations by "blood" (based on ideas common in their own cultures) later research<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schneider_p._182_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schneider_p._182-5">[5]</a></sup> has shown that many societies instead understand family through ideas of living together, the sharing of food (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Milk_kinship" title="Milk kinship">milk kinship</a>) and sharing care and <a href="/wiki/Nurture_kinship" title="Nurture kinship">nurture</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sociologist" title="Sociologist">Sociologists</a> have a special interest in the function and status of family forms in stratified (especially <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">capitalist</a>) societies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the work of scholars <a href="/wiki/Max_Weber" title="Max Weber">Max Weber</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alan_Macfarlane" title="Alan Macfarlane">Alan Macfarlane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Steven_Ozment" title="Steven Ozment">Steven Ozment</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jack_Goody" title="Jack Goody">Jack Goody</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Laslett" title="Peter Laslett">Peter Laslett</a>, the huge transformation that led to modern marriage in Western democracies was "fueled by the religio-cultural value system provided by elements of <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>, early <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a> <a href="/wiki/Canon_law_of_the_Catholic_Church" title="Canon law of the Catholic Church">canon law</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>Much sociological, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Historical" title="Historical">historical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cultural_anthropology" title="Cultural anthropology">anthropological</a> research dedicates itself to the understanding of this variation, and of changes in the family that form over time. Levitan claims:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Times have changed; it is more acceptable and encouraged for mothers to work and fathers to spend more time at home with the children. The way roles are balanced between the parents will help children grow and learn valuable life lessons. There is [the] great importance of communication and equality in families, in order to avoid role strain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Multigenerational_family">Multigenerational family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Multigenerational family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Historically, the most common family type was one in which grandparents, parents, and children lived together as a single unit.  For example, the household might include the owners of a farm, one (or more) of their adult children, the adult child's spouse, and the adult child's own children (the owners' grandchildren).  Members of the extended family are not included in this family group.  Sometimes, "skipped" generation families, such as a grandparents living with their grandchildren, are included.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Settled_sami_vasterbotten_sweden_publ_1926.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1126" data-file-width="1747" decoding="async" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Settled_sami_vasterbotten_sweden_publ_1926.jpg/220px-Settled_sami_vasterbotten_sweden_publ_1926.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Settled_sami_vasterbotten_sweden_publ_1926.jpg/330px-Settled_sami_vasterbotten_sweden_publ_1926.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Settled_sami_vasterbotten_sweden_publ_1926.jpg/440px-Settled_sami_vasterbotten_sweden_publ_1926.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Settled_sami_vasterbotten_sweden_publ_1926.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Settled Sami (Lapplander) family of farmers in <a href="/wiki/Stensele" title="Stensele">Stensele</a>, <a href="/wiki/V%C3%A4sterbotten" title="Västerbotten">Västerbotten</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, early 20th century</div></div></div>
<p>In the US, this arrangement declined after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, reaching a low point in 1980, when about one out of every eight people in the US lived in a multigenerational family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-20">[20]</a></sup>  The numbers have risen since then, with one in five people in the US living in a multigenerational family as of 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>  The increasing popularity is partly driven by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Demographic_change" title="Demographic change">demographic changes</a> and the economic shifts associated with the <a href="/wiki/Boomerang_Generation" title="Boomerang Generation">Boomerang Generation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>Multigenerational households are less common in Canada, where about 6% of people living in Canada were living in multigenerational families as of 2016, but the proportion of multigenerational households was increasing rapidly, driven by increasing numbers of Aboriginal families, immigrant families, and high housing costs in some regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Conjugal_.28nuclear.29_family"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Conjugal_(nuclear)_family">Conjugal (nuclear) family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Conjugal (nuclear) family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The term "<a href="/wiki/Nuclear_family" title="Nuclear family">nuclear family</a>" is commonly used to refer to conjugal families. A "<a href="/wiki/Marriage" title="Marriage">conjugal</a>" family includes only the spouses and unmarried children who are not of age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (June 2018)">failed verification</span></a></i>]</sup> Some sociologists<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (June 2018)">which?</span></a></i>]</sup> distinguish between conjugal families (relatively independent of the kindred of the parents and of other families in general) and nuclear families (which maintain relatively close ties with their kindred).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wm._Riley_Blankinship,_miner,_with_his_children._Koppers_Coal_Division,_Kopperston_Mine,_Kopperston,_Wyoming_County..._-_NARA_-_540984.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2393" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Wm._Riley_Blankinship%2C_miner%2C_with_his_children._Koppers_Coal_Division%2C_Kopperston_Mine%2C_Kopperston%2C_Wyoming_County..._-_NARA_-_540984.jpg/220px-Wm._Riley_Blankinship%2C_miner%2C_with_his_children._Koppers_Coal_Division%2C_Kopperston_Mine%2C_Kopperston%2C_Wyoming_County..._-_NARA_-_540984.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Wm._Riley_Blankinship%2C_miner%2C_with_his_children._Koppers_Coal_Division%2C_Kopperston_Mine%2C_Kopperston%2C_Wyoming_County..._-_NARA_-_540984.jpg/330px-Wm._Riley_Blankinship%2C_miner%2C_with_his_children._Koppers_Coal_Division%2C_Kopperston_Mine%2C_Kopperston%2C_Wyoming_County..._-_NARA_-_540984.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Wm._Riley_Blankinship%2C_miner%2C_with_his_children._Koppers_Coal_Division%2C_Kopperston_Mine%2C_Kopperston%2C_Wyoming_County..._-_NARA_-_540984.jpg/440px-Wm._Riley_Blankinship%2C_miner%2C_with_his_children._Koppers_Coal_Division%2C_Kopperston_Mine%2C_Kopperston%2C_Wyoming_County..._-_NARA_-_540984.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wm._Riley_Blankinship,_miner,_with_his_children._Koppers_Coal_Division,_Kopperston_Mine,_Kopperston,_Wyoming_County..._-_NARA_-_540984.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A father with his children in the United States in the 1940s</div></div></div>
<p>Other family structures – with (for example) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blended_families" title="Blended families">blended parents</a>, <a href="/wiki/Single_parent" title="Single parent">single parents</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Domestic_partnership" title="Domestic partnership">domestic partnerships</a> – have begun to challenge the normality of the nuclear family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Single-parent_family">Single-parent family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Single-parent family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Single-parent_family" title="Single-parent family">single-parent family</a></i> consists of one parent together with their children, where the parent is either widowed, divorced (and not remarried), or never married. The parent may have <a href="/wiki/Sole_custody" title="Sole custody">sole custody</a> of the children, or separated parents may have a <a href="/wiki/Shared_parenting" title="Shared parenting">shared-parenting</a> arrangement where the children divide their time (possibly equally) between two different single-parent families or between one single-parent family and one <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blended_family" title="Blended family">blended family</a>. As compared to sole custody, physical, mental and social well-being of children may be improved by shared-parenting arrangements and by children having greater access to both parents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> The number of single-parent families have been<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (October 2019)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> increasing, and about half of all children in the United States will live in a single-parent family at some point before they reach the age of 18. Most single-parent families are headed by a mother, but the number of single-parent families headed by fathers is increasing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Matrifocal_family">Matrifocal family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Matrifocal family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Matrifocal_family" title="Matrifocal family">Matrifocal family</a></div>
<p>A "matrifocal" family consists of a <a href="/wiki/Mother" title="Mother">mother</a> and her children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Randolph_1964_628–631_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Randolph_1964_628–631-33">[33]</a></sup> Generally, these children are her biological offspring, although adoption of children occurs in nearly every society. This kind of family occurs commonly where women have the resources to rear their children by themselves, or where men are more <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Mobility_(disambiguation)" title="Mobility (disambiguation)">mobile</a> than women. As a definition, "a family or domestic group is matrifocal when it is centred on a woman and her children. In this case, the father(s) of these children are intermittently present in the life of the group and occupy a secondary place. The children's mother is not necessarily the wife of one of the children's fathers."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> The name, matrifocal, was coined in Guiana but it is defined differently in other countries. For Nayar families, the family have the male as the "center" or the head of the family, either the step-father/father/brother, rather than the mother.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Randolph_1964_628–631_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Randolph_1964_628–631-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Extended_family">Extended family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Extended family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Family_eating_meal.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1800" data-file-width="2700" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Family_eating_meal.jpg/220px-Family_eating_meal.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Family_eating_meal.jpg/330px-Family_eating_meal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Family_eating_meal.jpg/440px-Family_eating_meal.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Family_eating_meal.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Hispanic" title="Hispanic">Hispanic</a> family eating a meal.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Basankusu_-_typical_fired_brick_house.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Basankusu_-_typical_fired_brick_house.jpg/220px-Basankusu_-_typical_fired_brick_house.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Basankusu_-_typical_fired_brick_house.jpg/330px-Basankusu_-_typical_fired_brick_house.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Basankusu_-_typical_fired_brick_house.jpg/440px-Basankusu_-_typical_fired_brick_house.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Basankusu_-_typical_fired_brick_house.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A family from Basankusu, <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Democratic Republic of the Congo</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coloured-family.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="630" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Coloured-family.jpg/220px-Coloured-family.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Coloured-family.jpg/330px-Coloured-family.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Coloured-family.jpg/440px-Coloured-family.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coloured-family.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Extended family with roots in Cape Town, Kimberley and Pretoria, South Africa</div></div></div>
<p>The term "<a href="/wiki/Extended_family" title="Extended family">extended family</a>" is also common, especially in the United States. This term has two distinct meanings:
</p>
<ol><li>It serves as a synonym of "consanguinal family" (consanguine means "of the same blood").</li>
<li>In societies dominated by the conjugal family, it refers to "<a href="/wiki/Kinship" title="Kinship">kindred</a>" (an egocentric network of relatives that extends beyond the domestic group) who do not belong to the conjugal family.</li></ol>
<p>These types refer to ideal or normative structures found in particular societies. Any society will exhibit some variation in the actual composition and conception of families.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>Historically, <i><a href="/wiki/Extended_family" title="Extended family">extended families</a></i> were the basic family unit in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholic_culture" title="Catholic culture">Catholic culture</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholic_countries" title="Catholic countries">countries</a> (such as  <a href="/wiki/Southern_Europe" title="Southern Europe">Southern Europe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pritchard_2006_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pritchard_2006-36">[36]</a></sup> and in <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asian</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Middle_Eastern" title="Middle Eastern">Middle Eastern</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodoxy_by_country" title="Eastern Orthodoxy by country">Eastern Orthodox</a> countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pritchard_2006_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pritchard_2006-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_of_choice">Family of choice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Family of choice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The term <a href="/wiki/Family_of_choice" title="Family of choice">family of choice</a>, also sometimes referred to as "chosen family" or "found family", is common within the <a href="/wiki/LGBT_community" title="LGBT community">LGBT community</a>, veterans, individuals who have suffered abuse, and those who have no contact with biological "parents". It refers to the group of people in an individual's life that satisfies the typical role of family as a support system. The term differentiates between the "family of origin" (the biological family or that in which people are raised) and those that actively assume that ideal role.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>The family of choice may or may not include some or all of the members of the family of origin. This family is not one that follows the "normal" familial structure like having a father, a mother, and children. This is family is a group of people that rely on each other like a family of origin would.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> This terminology stems from the fact that many <a href="/wiki/LGBT" title="LGBT">LGBT</a> individuals, upon <a href="/wiki/Coming_out" title="Coming out">coming out</a>, face rejection or shame from the families they were raised in.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stitt_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stitt-39">[39]</a></sup> The term family of choice is also used by individuals in the 12 step communities, who create close-knit "family" ties through the recovery process.
</p><p>As a family system, families of choice face unique issues. Without legal safeguards, families of choice may struggle when medical, educational or governmental institutions fail to recognize their legitimacy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stitt_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stitt-39">[39]</a></sup> If members of the chosen family have been disowned by their family of origin, they may experience surrogate grief, displacing anger, loss, or anxious attachment onto their new family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stitt_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-Stitt-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Blended_family">Blended family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Blended family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The term <i>blended family</i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Stepfamily" title="Stepfamily">stepfamily</a></i> describes families with mixed parents: one or both parents remarried, bringing children of the former family into the new family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Also in sociology, particularly in the works of social psychologist <a href="/wiki/Michael_Lamb_(psychologist)" title="Michael Lamb (psychologist)">Michael Lamb</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> <i>traditional family</i> refers to "a middle-class family with a bread-winning father and a stay-at-home mother, married to each other and raising their biological children," and <i>nontraditional</i> to exceptions to this rule. Most of the US households are now non-traditional under this definition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Critics of the term "traditional family" point out that in most cultures and at most times, the <a href="/wiki/Extended_family" title="Extended family">extended family</a> model has been most common, not the nuclear family,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> though it has had a longer tradition in England<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> than in other parts of Europe and Asia which contributed large numbers of immigrants to the Americas. The nuclear family became the most common form in the U.S. in the 1960s and 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>In terms of communication patterns in families, there are a certain set of beliefs within the family that reflect how its members should communicate and interact. These family communication patterns arise from two underlying sets of beliefs. One being conversation orientation (the degree to which the importance of communication is valued) and two, conformity orientation (the degree to which families should emphasize similarities or differences regarding attitudes, beliefs, and values).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>Blended families is complex, ranging from stepfamilies to cohabitating families (an individual living with guardians who are not married with step or half siblings). While it's not too different from stepfamilies, cohabiting families pose a prevalent psychological effect on youths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Some adolescents would be prone to "acts of delinquency," and experiencing problems in school ranging from a decrease in academic performance to increased problematic behavior.  It coincides with other researches on the trajectories of stepfamilies where some experienced familyhood, but others lacking connection. Emotional detachment from members within stepfamilies contributes to this uncertainty, furthering the tension that these families may establish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> The transition from an old family to a new family that falls under blended families would also become problematic as the activities that were once performed in the old family may not transfer well within the new family for adolescents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Monogamous_family">Monogamous family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Monogamous family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A monogamous family is based on a legal or social <a href="/wiki/Monogamy" title="Monogamy">monogamy</a>. In this case, an individual has only one (official) partner during their lifetime or at any one time (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Monogamy#Serial_monogamy" title="Monogamy">serial monogamy</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BRIT_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-BRIT-50">[50]</a></sup> This means that a person may not have several different legal spouses at the same time, as this is usually prohibited by <a href="/wiki/Bigamy" title="Bigamy">bigamy</a> laws, (the act of entering into a marriage with one person while still legally married to another<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>) in jurisdictions that require monogamous marriages.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polygamous_family">Polygamous family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Polygamous family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kwong_Sue_Duk_with_his_three_wives_and_fourteen_children,_Cairns,_1904_(9623512597).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="711" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Kwong_Sue_Duk_with_his_three_wives_and_fourteen_children%2C_Cairns%2C_1904_%289623512597%29.jpg/220px-Kwong_Sue_Duk_with_his_three_wives_and_fourteen_children%2C_Cairns%2C_1904_%289623512597%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Kwong_Sue_Duk_with_his_three_wives_and_fourteen_children%2C_Cairns%2C_1904_%289623512597%29.jpg/330px-Kwong_Sue_Duk_with_his_three_wives_and_fourteen_children%2C_Cairns%2C_1904_%289623512597%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Kwong_Sue_Duk_with_his_three_wives_and_fourteen_children%2C_Cairns%2C_1904_%289623512597%29.jpg/440px-Kwong_Sue_Duk_with_his_three_wives_and_fourteen_children%2C_Cairns%2C_1904_%289623512597%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kwong_Sue_Duk_with_his_three_wives_and_fourteen_children,_Cairns,_1904_(9623512597).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chinese immigrant with his three wives and fourteen children, <a href="/wiki/Cairns" title="Cairns">Cairns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, 1904</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Polygamy" title="Polygamy">Polygamy</a> is a marriage that includes more than two partners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zeitzen_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zeitzen-53">[53]</a></sup> When a man is married to more than one wife at a time, the relationship is called <a href="/wiki/Polygyny" title="Polygyny">polygyny</a>; and when a woman is married to more than one husband at a time, it is called <a href="/wiki/Polyandry" title="Polyandry">polyandry</a>. If a marriage includes multiple husbands and wives, it can be called <a href="/wiki/Polyamory" title="Polyamory">polyamory</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Group_marriage" title="Group marriage">group or conjoint marriage</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zeitzen_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zeitzen-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Polygyny" title="Polygyny">Polygyny</a> is a form of plural marriage, in which a man is allowed more than one wife .<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> In modern countries that permit polygamy, polygyny is typically the only form permitted. Polygyny is practiced primarily (but not only) in parts of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> and <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>; and is often associated with <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, however, there are certain conditions in Islam that must be met to perform polygyny.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Polyandry" title="Polyandry">Polyandry</a> is a form of marriage whereby a woman takes two or more husbands at the same time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Starkweather/Hames_2012_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Starkweather/Hames_2012-57">[57]</a></sup> Fraternal polyandry, where two or more brothers are married to the same wife, is a common form of polyandry. Polyandry was traditionally practiced in areas of the Himalayan mountains, among Tibetans in <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>, in parts of <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> and in parts of northern <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>. Polyandry is most common in societies marked by high male mortality or where males will often be apart from the rest of the family for a considerable period of time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Starkweather/Hames_2012_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Starkweather/Hames_2012-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Kinship_terminology">Kinship terminology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Kinship terminology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Degrees_of_kinship">Degrees of kinship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Degrees of kinship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coefficient_of_relationship" title="Coefficient of relationship">Coefficient of relationship</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Albumen_photograph_of_an_Indian_family_with_children_in_the_1870s.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="570" data-file-width="421" decoding="async" height="271" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Albumen_photograph_of_an_Indian_family_with_children_in_the_1870s.jpg/200px-Albumen_photograph_of_an_Indian_family_with_children_in_the_1870s.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Albumen_photograph_of_an_Indian_family_with_children_in_the_1870s.jpg/300px-Albumen_photograph_of_an_Indian_family_with_children_in_the_1870s.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Albumen_photograph_of_an_Indian_family_with_children_in_the_1870s.jpg/400px-Albumen_photograph_of_an_Indian_family_with_children_in_the_1870s.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Albumen_photograph_of_an_Indian_family_with_children_in_the_1870s.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Family in India, 1870s</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arthur_Rothstein_Family_in_a_wagon_Lee_County_August_1935.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1164" data-file-width="1432" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Arthur_Rothstein_Family_in_a_wagon_Lee_County_August_1935.jpg/200px-Arthur_Rothstein_Family_in_a_wagon_Lee_County_August_1935.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Arthur_Rothstein_Family_in_a_wagon_Lee_County_August_1935.jpg/300px-Arthur_Rothstein_Family_in_a_wagon_Lee_County_August_1935.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Arthur_Rothstein_Family_in_a_wagon_Lee_County_August_1935.jpg/400px-Arthur_Rothstein_Family_in_a_wagon_Lee_County_August_1935.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arthur_Rothstein_Family_in_a_wagon_Lee_County_August_1935.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Family in a wagon, Lee County, Mississippi, United States, August 1935.</div></div></div>
<p>A first-degree relative is one who shares 50% of your DNA through direct inheritance, such as a full sibling, parent or progeny.
</p><p>There is another measure for the degree of relationship, which is determined by counting up generations to the first common ancestor and back down to the target individual, which is used for various genealogical and legal purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminologies">Terminologies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Terminologies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kinship_terminology" title="Kinship terminology">Kinship terminology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Family_(version_1).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="782" data-file-width="3728" decoding="async" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Family_%28version_1%29.png/500px-Family_%28version_1%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Family_%28version_1%29.png/750px-Family_%28version_1%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Family_%28version_1%29.png/1000px-Family_%28version_1%29.png 2x" width="500"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Family_(version_1).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Family tree with some family members.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Family_-_version_2.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="780" data-file-width="3856" decoding="async" height="101" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Family_-_version_2.png/500px-Family_-_version_2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Family_-_version_2.png/750px-Family_-_version_2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Family_-_version_2.png/1000px-Family_-_version_2.png 2x" width="500"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Family_-_version_2.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Family tree with other family members.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Middagsm%C3%A5ltid_i_folkstugan._Oxie_h%C3%A4rad,_V%C3%A4stra_Klagstorps_socken,_Sk%C3%A5ne_-_Nordiska_Museet_-_NMA.0033405.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1073" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Middagsm%C3%A5ltid_i_folkstugan._Oxie_h%C3%A4rad%2C_V%C3%A4stra_Klagstorps_socken%2C_Sk%C3%A5ne_-_Nordiska_Museet_-_NMA.0033405.jpg/220px-Middagsm%C3%A5ltid_i_folkstugan._Oxie_h%C3%A4rad%2C_V%C3%A4stra_Klagstorps_socken%2C_Sk%C3%A5ne_-_Nordiska_Museet_-_NMA.0033405.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Middagsm%C3%A5ltid_i_folkstugan._Oxie_h%C3%A4rad%2C_V%C3%A4stra_Klagstorps_socken%2C_Sk%C3%A5ne_-_Nordiska_Museet_-_NMA.0033405.jpg/330px-Middagsm%C3%A5ltid_i_folkstugan._Oxie_h%C3%A4rad%2C_V%C3%A4stra_Klagstorps_socken%2C_Sk%C3%A5ne_-_Nordiska_Museet_-_NMA.0033405.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Middagsm%C3%A5ltid_i_folkstugan._Oxie_h%C3%A4rad%2C_V%C3%A4stra_Klagstorps_socken%2C_Sk%C3%A5ne_-_Nordiska_Museet_-_NMA.0033405.jpg/440px-Middagsm%C3%A5ltid_i_folkstugan._Oxie_h%C3%A4rad%2C_V%C3%A4stra_Klagstorps_socken%2C_Sk%C3%A5ne_-_Nordiska_Museet_-_NMA.0033405.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Middagsm%C3%A5ltid_i_folkstugan._Oxie_h%C3%A4rad,_V%C3%A4stra_Klagstorps_socken,_Sk%C3%A5ne_-_Nordiska_Museet_-_NMA.0033405.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Swedish family eating, 1902</div></div></div>
<p>In his book <i><a href="/wiki/Systems_of_Consanguinity_and_Affinity_of_the_Human_Family" title="Systems of Consanguinity and Affinity of the Human Family">Systems of Consanguinity and Affinity of the Human Family</a></i>, anthropologist <a href="/wiki/Lewis_H._Morgan" title="Lewis H. Morgan">Lewis Henry Morgan</a> (1818–1881) performed the first survey of kinship terminologies in use around the world. Although much of his work is now considered dated, he argued that <a href="/wiki/Kinship" title="Kinship">kinship</a> terminologies reflect different sets of distinctions. For example, most kinship terminologies distinguish between sexes (the difference between a brother and a sister) and between generations (the difference between a child and a parent). Moreover, he argued, kinship terminologies distinguish between relatives by blood and <a href="/wiki/Marriage" title="Marriage">marriage</a> (although recently some anthropologists have argued that many societies define kinship in terms other than "blood").
</p><p>Morgan made a distinction between kinship systems that use <i>classificatory</i> terminology and those that use <i>descriptive</i> terminology. Classificatory systems are generally and erroneously understood to be those that "class together" with a single term relatives who actually do not have the same type of relationship to ego. (What defines "same type of relationship" under such definitions seems to be genealogical relationship. This is problematic given that any genealogical description, no matter how standardized, employs words originating in a folk understanding of kinship.) What Morgan's terminology actually differentiates are those (classificatory) kinship systems that do not distinguish lineal and collateral relationships and those (descriptive) kinship systems that do. Morgan, a lawyer, came to make this distinction in an effort to understand <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seneca_tribe" title="Seneca tribe">Seneca</a> inheritance practices. A Seneca man's effects were inherited by his sisters' children rather than by his own children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Morgan identified six basic patterns of kinship terminologies:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_kinship" title="Hawaiian kinship">Hawaiian</a>: only distinguishes relatives based upon sex and generation.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sudanese_kinship" title="Sudanese kinship">Sudanese</a>: no two relatives share the same term.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Eskimo_kinship" title="Eskimo kinship">Eskimo</a>: in addition to distinguishing relatives based upon sex and generation, also distinguishes between lineal relatives and collateral relatives.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Iroquois_kinship" title="Iroquois kinship">Iroquois</a>: in addition to sex and generation, also distinguishes between siblings of opposite sexes in the parental generation.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Crow_kinship" title="Crow kinship">Crow</a>: a matrilineal system with some features of an Iroquois system, but with a "skewing" feature in which generation is "frozen" for some relatives.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Omaha_kinship" title="Omaha kinship">Omaha</a>: like a Crow system but patrilineal.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Roles">Roles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Roles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Grandson" redirects here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Grandson_(disambiguation)" title="Grandson (disambiguation)">Grandson (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Paus_family_portrait_NFB-18645.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2967" data-file-width="2400" decoding="async" height="272" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Paus_family_portrait_NFB-18645.jpg/220px-Paus_family_portrait_NFB-18645.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Paus_family_portrait_NFB-18645.jpg/330px-Paus_family_portrait_NFB-18645.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Paus_family_portrait_NFB-18645.jpg/440px-Paus_family_portrait_NFB-18645.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Paus_family_portrait_NFB-18645.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Group photograph of a Norwegian family by <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Borgen" title="Gustav Borgen">Gustav Borgen</a> ca. 1900: Father, mother, three sons and two daughters.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bj%C3%B8rnstjerne_Bj%C3%B8rnson_og_barnebarnet_Bj%C3%B8rnstjerne_Albert_Bj%C3%B8rnson-Langen,_1900.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="677" decoding="async" height="333" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Bj%C3%B8rnstjerne_Bj%C3%B8rnson_og_barnebarnet_Bj%C3%B8rnstjerne_Albert_Bj%C3%B8rnson-Langen%2C_1900.jpg/220px-Bj%C3%B8rnstjerne_Bj%C3%B8rnson_og_barnebarnet_Bj%C3%B8rnstjerne_Albert_Bj%C3%B8rnson-Langen%2C_1900.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Bj%C3%B8rnstjerne_Bj%C3%B8rnson_og_barnebarnet_Bj%C3%B8rnstjerne_Albert_Bj%C3%B8rnson-Langen%2C_1900.jpg/330px-Bj%C3%B8rnstjerne_Bj%C3%B8rnson_og_barnebarnet_Bj%C3%B8rnstjerne_Albert_Bj%C3%B8rnson-Langen%2C_1900.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Bj%C3%B8rnstjerne_Bj%C3%B8rnson_og_barnebarnet_Bj%C3%B8rnstjerne_Albert_Bj%C3%B8rnson-Langen%2C_1900.jpg/440px-Bj%C3%B8rnstjerne_Bj%C3%B8rnson_og_barnebarnet_Bj%C3%B8rnstjerne_Albert_Bj%C3%B8rnson-Langen%2C_1900.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bj%C3%B8rnstjerne_Bj%C3%B8rnson_og_barnebarnet_Bj%C3%B8rnstjerne_Albert_Bj%C3%B8rnson-Langen,_1900.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bj%C3%B8rnstjerne_Bj%C3%B8rnson" title="Bjørnstjerne Bjørnson">Bjørnstjerne Bjørnson</a> with grandchild, 1900</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Father_and_child,_Dhaka.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="720" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Father_and_child%2C_Dhaka.jpg/220px-Father_and_child%2C_Dhaka.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Father_and_child%2C_Dhaka.jpg/330px-Father_and_child%2C_Dhaka.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Father_and_child%2C_Dhaka.jpg/440px-Father_and_child%2C_Dhaka.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Father_and_child,_Dhaka.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Father and child, Dhaka, Bangladesh</div></div></div>
<p>Most Western societies employ <a href="/wiki/Eskimo_kinship" title="Eskimo kinship">Eskimo kinship</a> terminology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> This kinship terminology commonly occurs in societies with strong <a href="/wiki/Conjugal_family" title="Conjugal family">conjugal</a>, where families have a degree of relative mobility. Typically, societies with conjugal families also favor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neolocal" title="Neolocal">neolocal</a> residence; thus upon marriage, a person separates from the nuclear family of their childhood (family of orientation) and forms a new nuclear family (family of procreation). Such systems generally assume that the mother's husband is also the biological father. The system uses highly descriptive terms for the nuclear family and progressively more <a href="/wiki/Classificatory_kinship" title="Classificatory kinship">classificatory</a> as the relatives become more and more collateral.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_family">Nuclear family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Nuclear family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_family" title="Nuclear family">Nuclear family</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Juho_kusti_paasikivi_family_1906.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="246" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Juho_kusti_paasikivi_family_1906.jpg/220px-Juho_kusti_paasikivi_family_1906.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Juho_kusti_paasikivi_family_1906.jpg/330px-Juho_kusti_paasikivi_family_1906.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8e/Juho_kusti_paasikivi_family_1906.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Juho_kusti_paasikivi_family_1906.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The family of Finnish statesman <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J._K._Paasikivi" title="J. K. Paasikivi">J. K. Paasikivi</a> (<i>right</i>) in 1906</div></div></div>
<p>The system emphasizes the nuclear family. Members of the nuclear family use highly descriptive kinship terms, identifying directly only the husband, wife, mother, father, son, daughter, brother, and sister. All other relatives are grouped together into categories. Members of the nuclear family may be lineal or collateral. Kin, for whom these are family, refer to them in descriptive terms that build on the terms used within the nuclear family or use the nuclear family term directly.
</p><p>Nuclear family of orientation
</p>
<ul><li><b><a href="/wiki/Brother" title="Brother">Brother</a></b>: the male child of a parent.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Sister" title="Sister">Sister</a></b>: the female child of a parent.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Father" title="Father">Father</a></b>: a male parent.
<ul><li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grandfather" title="Grandfather">Grandfather</a></b>: the father of a parent.</li></ul></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Mother" title="Mother">Mother</a></b>: a female parent.
<ul><li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grandmother" title="Grandmother">Grandmother</a></b>: the mother of a parent.</li></ul></li></ul>
<p>Nuclear conjugal family
</p>
<ul><li><b><a href="/wiki/Husband" title="Husband">Husband</a></b>: a male spouse.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Wife" title="Wife">Wife</a></b>: a female spouse.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Son" title="Son">Son</a></b>: a male child of the parent(s).
<ul><li><b>Grandson</b>: a child's son.</li></ul></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Daughter" title="Daughter">Daughter</a></b>: a female child of the parent(s).
<ul><li><b>Granddaughter</b>: a child's daughter.</li></ul></li></ul>
<p>Nuclear non-lineal family
</p>
<ul><li><b><a href="/wiki/Spouse" title="Spouse">Spouse</a></b>: husband or wife
<ul><li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stepparent" title="Stepparent">Stepparent</a></b>: a spouse of a parent that is not a biological parent</li></ul></li>
<li><b>Sibling</b>: sister or brother
<ul><li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Half-sibling" title="Half-sibling">Half-sibling</a></b>: a sibling with whom the subject shares only one biological parent</li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Step-sibling" title="Step-sibling">Step-sibling</a></b>: a child of a parent that is not a biological parent</li></ul></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Collateral_relatives">Collateral relatives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Collateral relatives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A sibling is a collateral relative with a minimal removal. For collateral relatives with one additional removal, one generation more distant from a common ancestor on one side, more classificatory terms come into play. These terms (<a href="/wiki/Aunt" title="Aunt">Aunt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uncle" title="Uncle">Uncle</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Niece" title="Niece">Niece</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nephew" title="Nephew">Nephew</a>) do not build on the terms used within the nuclear family as most are not traditionally members of the household. These terms do not traditionally differentiate between a collateral relatives and a person married to a collateral relative (both collateral and aggregate). Collateral relatives with additional removals on each side are <a href="/wiki/Cousin" title="Cousin">Cousins</a>. This is the most classificatory term and can be distinguished by <b>degrees of collaterality</b> and by generation (<b>removal</b>).
</p><p>When only the subject has the additional removal, the relative is the subject's parents' siblings, the terms <b>Aunt</b> and <b>Uncle</b> are used for female and male relatives respectively. When only the relative has the additional removal, the relative is the subjects siblings child, the terms <b>Niece</b> and <b>Nephew</b> are used for female and male relatives respectively. The spouse of a biological aunt or uncle is an aunt or uncle, and the nieces and nephews of a spouse are nieces and nephews. With further removal by the subject for aunts and uncles and by the relative for nieces and nephews the prefix "grand-" modifies these terms. With further removal the prefix becomes "great-grand-," adding another "great-" for each additional generation. For large numbers of generations a number can be substituted, for example, "fourth great-grandson", "four-greats grandson" or "four-times-great-grandson".
</p><p>When the subject and the relative have an additional removal they are cousins. A cousin with minimal removal is a first cousin, i.e. the child of the subjects uncle or aunt. <b>Degrees of collaterality</b> and <b>removals</b> are used to more precisely describe the relationship between cousins. The <b>degree</b> is the number of generations subsequent to the common ancestor before a parent of one of the cousins is found, while the <b>removal</b> is the difference between the number of generations from each cousin to the common ancestor (the difference between the generations the cousins are from).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DictGen_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-DictGen-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DictWebster_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-DictWebster-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cousins of an older generation (in other words, one's parents' first cousins), although technically first cousins once removed, are often classified with "aunts" and "uncles".
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aggregate_relatives">Aggregate relatives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Aggregate relatives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>English-speakers mark relationships by marriage (except for wife/husband) with the tag "-in-law". The mother and father of one's spouse become one's mother-in-law and father-in-law; the wife of one's son becomes one's daughter-in-law and the husband of one's daughter becomes one's son-in-law. The term "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sister-in-law" title="Sister-in-law">sister-in-law</a>" refers to two essentially different relationships, either the wife of one's brother, or the sister of one's spouse. "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brother-in-law" title="Brother-in-law">Brother-in-law</a>" is the husband of one's sister, or the brother of one's spouse. The terms "half-brother" and "half-sister" indicate siblings who share only one biological parent. The term "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aunt-in-law" title="Aunt-in-law">aunt-in-law</a>" is the wife of one's uncle, or the aunt of one's spouse. "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uncle-in-law" title="Uncle-in-law">Uncle-in-law</a>" is the husband of one's aunt, or the uncle of one's spouse. "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cousin-in-law" title="Cousin-in-law">Cousin-in-law</a>" is the spouse of one's cousin, or the cousin of one's spouse. The term "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Niece-in-law" title="Niece-in-law">niece-in-law</a>" is the wife of one's nephew, or the niece of one's spouse. "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nephew-in-law" title="Nephew-in-law">Nephew-in-law</a>" is the husband of one's niece, or the nephew of one's spouse. The grandmother and grandfather of one's spouse become one's grandmother-in-law and grandfather-in-law; the wife of one's grandson becomes one's granddaughter-in-law and the husband of one's granddaughter becomes one's grandson-in-law.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Indian_English" title="Indian English">Indian English</a> a sibling in law who is the spouse of your sibling can be referred to as a co-sibling (specificity a co-sister<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ca-CoS_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ca-CoS-65">[65]</a></sup> or co-brother<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ca-CoB_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ca-CoB-66">[66]</a></sup>).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types_of_kinship">Types of kinship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Types of kinship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Patrilineal">Patrilineal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Patrilineal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Patrilineality" title="Patrilineality">Patrilineality</a>, also known as <i>the male line</i> or <i>agnatic kinship</i>, is a form of kinship system in which an individual's family membership derives from and is traced through his or her father's <a href="/wiki/Lineage_(anthropology)" title="Lineage (anthropology)">lineage</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> It generally involves the <a href="/wiki/Inheritance" title="Inheritance">inheritance</a> of property, rights, names, or titles by persons related through <a href="/wiki/Male" title="Male">male</a> kin.
</p><p>A patriline ("father line") is a person's father, and additional <a href="/wiki/Ancestor" title="Ancestor">ancestors</a> that are traced only through males. One's patriline is thus a record of descent from a man in which the individuals in all intervening generations are male. In <a href="/wiki/Cultural_anthropology" title="Cultural anthropology">cultural anthropology</a>, a patrilineage is a <a href="/wiki/Consanguinity" title="Consanguinity">consanguineal</a> male and female <a href="/wiki/Kinship" title="Kinship">kinship group</a>, each of whose members is descended from the common ancestor through male forebears.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Matrilineal">Matrilineal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Matrilineal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Queen_Victoria_the_Princess_Royal_Victoria_c1844-5.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="1153" decoding="async" height="286" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Queen_Victoria_the_Princess_Royal_Victoria_c1844-5.png/220px-Queen_Victoria_the_Princess_Royal_Victoria_c1844-5.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Queen_Victoria_the_Princess_Royal_Victoria_c1844-5.png/330px-Queen_Victoria_the_Princess_Royal_Victoria_c1844-5.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Queen_Victoria_the_Princess_Royal_Victoria_c1844-5.png/440px-Queen_Victoria_the_Princess_Royal_Victoria_c1844-5.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Queen_Victoria_the_Princess_Royal_Victoria_c1844-5.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Queen Victoria</a>, with her eldest daughter</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Matrilineality" title="Matrilineality">Matrilineality</a> is a form of kinship system in which an individual's family membership derives from and is traced through his or her mother's <a href="/wiki/Lineage_(anthropology)" title="Lineage (anthropology)">lineage</a>.
</p><p>It may also correlate with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Societal_system" title="Societal system">societal system</a> in which each person is identified with their matriline—their mother's <a href="/wiki/Lineage_(anthropology)" title="Lineage (anthropology)">lineage</a>—and which can involve the <a href="/wiki/Inheritance" title="Inheritance">inheritance</a> of property and titles. A matriline is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Line_of_descent" title="Line of descent">line of descent</a> from a <a href="/wiki/Female" title="Female">female</a> <a href="/wiki/Ancestor" title="Ancestor">ancestor</a> to a descendant in which the individuals in all intervening generations are mothers – in other words, a "mother line".
</p><p>In a matrilineal descent system, an individual is considered to belong to the same <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Descent_group" title="Descent group">descent group</a> as her or his mother. This matrilineal descent pattern is in contrasts to the more common pattern of patrilineal descent pattern.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bilateral_descent">Bilateral descent</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Bilateral descent">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Bilateral_descent" title="Bilateral descent">Bilateral descent</a> is a form of kinship system in which an individual's family membership derives from and is traced through both the paternal and maternal sides. The relatives on the mother's side and father's side are equally important for emotional ties or for transfer of property or wealth. It is a family arrangement where descent and inheritance are passed equally through both parents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Families who use this system trace descent through both parents simultaneously and recognize multiple ancestors, but unlike with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cognatic" title="Cognatic">cognatic</a> descent it is not used to form descent groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>Traditionally, this is found among some groups in <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Melanesia" title="Melanesia">Melanesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polynesia" title="Polynesia">Polynesia</a>. Anthropologists believe that a tribal structure based on bilateral descent helps members live in extreme environments because it allows individuals to rely on two sets of families dispersed over a wide area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scientificamerican2001_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-scientificamerican2001-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_theories">History of theories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: History of theories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_family" title="History of the family">History of the family</a></div>
<p>Early scholars of family history applied <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Darwin</a>'s biological <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theory_of_evolution" title="Theory of evolution">theory of evolution</a> in their theory of evolution of family systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclopædia_Britannica_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopædia_Britannica-71">[71]</a></sup> American anthropologist <a href="/wiki/Lewis_H._Morgan" title="Lewis H. Morgan">Lewis H.
