Morgan</a> published <i><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Society" title="Ancient Society">Ancient Society</a></i> in 1877 based on his theory of the three stages of human progress from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primitive_culture" title="Primitive culture">Savagery</a> through <a href="/wiki/Barbarian" title="Barbarian">Barbarism</a> to <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">Civilization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Morgan's book was the "inspiration for <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Engels" title="Friedrich Engels">Friedrich Engels</a>' book" <i><a href="/wiki/The_Origin_of_the_Family,_Private_Property_and_the_State" title="The Origin of the Family, Private Property and the State">The Origin of the Family, Private Property and the State</a></i> published in 1884.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>Engels expanded Morgan's hypothesis that economical factors caused the transformation of primitive community into a class-divided society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Engels' theory of <a href="/wiki/Resource" title="Resource">resource</a> control, and later that of <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a>, was used to explain the cause and effect of change in family structure and function. The popularity of this theory was largely unmatched until the 1980s, when other sociological theories, most notably <a href="/wiki/Structural_functionalism" title="Structural functionalism">structural functionalism</a>, gained acceptance.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_nuclear_family_in_industrial_society">The nuclear family in industrial society</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: The nuclear family in industrial society">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Families_US.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="530" data-file-width="630" decoding="async" height="252" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Families_US.svg/300px-Families_US.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Families_US.svg/450px-Families_US.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Families_US.svg/600px-Families_US.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Families_US.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Family arrangements in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> have become more diverse with no particular household arrangement representing half of the United States population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marriages,_Families_&amp;_Intimate_Relationships_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marriages,_Families_&amp;_Intimate_Relationships-75">[75]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Contemporary society generally views the family as a haven from the world, supplying absolute fulfillment. Zinn and Eitzen discuss the image of the "family as haven ... a place of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intimacy" title="Intimacy">intimacy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Love" title="Love">love</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trust_(social_sciences)" title="Trust (social sciences)">trust</a> where individuals may <a href="/wiki/Escapism" title="Escapism">escape</a> the competition of dehumanizing forces in modern society".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>During <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Industrialization" title="Industrialization">industrialization</a>, "[t]he family as a repository of warmth and tenderness (embodied by the mother) stands in opposition to the competitive and aggressive world of commerce (embodied by the father). The family's task was to protect against the outside world."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZinnPg.3_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-ZinnPg.3-77">[77]</a></sup> However, Zinn and Eitzen note, "The protective image of the family has waned in recent years as the ideals of family fulfillment have taken shape. Today, the family is more compensatory than protective. It supplies what is vitally needed but missing in other social arrangements."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZinnPg.3_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-ZinnPg.3-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>"The popular wisdom", according to Zinn and Eitzen, sees the family structures of the past as superior to those today, and families as more stable and happier at a time when they did not have to contend with problems such as illegitimate children and <a href="/wiki/Divorce" title="Divorce">divorce</a>. They respond to this, saying, "there is no golden age of the family gleaming at us in the far back historical past."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZinnPg.8_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-ZinnPg.8-78">[78]</a></sup> "Desertion by spouses, illegitimate children, and other conditions that are considered characteristics of modern times existed in the past as well."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZinnPg.8_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-ZinnPg.8-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_postmodern_family">The postmodern family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: The postmodern family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Percentage_of_birth_to_unmarried_women,_selected_countries,_1980_and_2007.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="571" data-file-width="960" decoding="async" height="268" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Percentage_of_birth_to_unmarried_women%2C_selected_countries%2C_1980_and_2007.png/450px-Percentage_of_birth_to_unmarried_women%2C_selected_countries%2C_1980_and_2007.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Percentage_of_birth_to_unmarried_women%2C_selected_countries%2C_1980_and_2007.png/675px-Percentage_of_birth_to_unmarried_women%2C_selected_countries%2C_1980_and_2007.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Percentage_of_birth_to_unmarried_women%2C_selected_countries%2C_1980_and_2007.png/900px-Percentage_of_birth_to_unmarried_women%2C_selected_countries%2C_1980_and_2007.png 2x" width="450"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Percentage_of_birth_to_unmarried_women,_selected_countries,_1980_and_2007.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Percentage of births to unmarried women, selected countries, 1980 and 2007<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-non_mar1_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-non_mar1-79">[79]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Others argue that whether or not one views the family as "declining" depends on one's definition of "family". "Married couples have dropped below half of all American households. This drop is shocking from traditional forms of the family system. Only a fifth of households were following traditional ways of having married couples raising a family together."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> In the Western World, marriages are no longer <a href="/wiki/Arranged_marriage" title="Arranged marriage">arranged</a> for economic, social or political gain, and children are no longer expected to contribute to family income. Instead, people choose mates based on <a href="/wiki/Love" title="Love">love</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> This increased role of love indicates a societal shift toward favoring emotional fulfilment and relationships within a family, and this shift necessarily weakens the institution of the family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>Margaret Mead considers the family as a main safeguard to continuing human progress. Observing, "Human beings have learned, laboriously, to be human", she adds: "we hold our present form of humanity on trust, [and] it is possible to lose it" ... "It is not without significance that the most successful large-scale abrogations of the family have occurred not among simple savages, living close to the subsistence edge, but among great nations and strong empires, the resources of which were ample, the populations huge, and the power almost unlimited"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many countries (particularly Western) have, in recent years, changed their <a href="/wiki/Family_law" title="Family law">family laws</a> in order to accommodate diverse family models. For instance, in the United Kingdom, in <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, the <i>Family Law (Scotland) Act 2006</i> provides cohabitants with some limited rights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> In 2010, Ireland enacted the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Partnership_and_Certain_Rights_and_Obligations_of_Cohabitants_Act_2010" title="Civil Partnership and Certain Rights and Obligations of Cohabitants Act 2010">Civil Partnership and Certain Rights and Obligations of Cohabitants Act 2010</a>. There have also been moves at an international level, most notably, the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Europe" title="Council of Europe">Council of Europe</a> <i>European Convention on the Legal Status of Children Born out of Wedlock</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> which came into force in 1978. Countries which ratify it must ensure that children born outside marriage are provided with legal rights as stipulated in the text of this convention. The convention was ratified by the UK in 1981 and by Ireland in 1988.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United States, one in five mothers has children by different fathers; among mothers with two or more children the figure is higher, with 28% having children with at least two different men. Such families are more common among Blacks and Hispanics and among the lower socioeconomic class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, in western society, the single parent family has been growing more accepted and has begun to make an impact on culture. Single parent families are more commonly single mother families than single father.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> These families sometimes face difficult issues besides the fact that they have to rear their children on their own, for example, low income making it difficult to pay for rent, child care, and other necessities for a healthy and safe home.
</p><p>Furthermore, there are families that consist of two mothers, two fathers, non-binary, trans, and queer folks raising children.  This is made possible due to surrogacy, IVF, IUI, adoption, and other processes.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic_violence">Domestic violence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Domestic violence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Domestic_violence" title="Domestic violence">Domestic violence</a></div>
<p>Domestic violence (DV) is violence that happens within the family. The legal and social understanding of the concept of DV differs by culture. The definition of the term "domestic violence" varies, depending on the context in which it is used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> It may be defined differently in medical, legal, political or social contexts. The definitions have varied over time, and vary in different parts of the world.
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Convention_on_preventing_and_combating_violence_against_women_and_domestic_violence" title="Convention on preventing and combating violence against women and domestic violence">Convention on preventing and combating violence against women and domestic violence</a> states that:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>"domestic violence" shall mean all acts of physical, sexual, psychological or economic violence that occur within the family or domestic unit or between former or current spouses or partners, whether or not the perpetrator shares or has shared the same residence with the victim.</p></blockquote>
<p>In 1993, the United Nations <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Declaration_on_the_Elimination_of_Violence_against_Women" title="Declaration on the Elimination of Violence against Women">Declaration on the Elimination of Violence against Women</a> identified domestic violence as one of three contexts in which <a href="/wiki/Violence_against_women" title="Violence against women">violence against women</a> occurs, describing it as:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Physical, sexual and psychological violence occurring in the family, including battering, sexual abuse of female children in the household, <a href="/wiki/Dowry" title="Dowry">dowry</a>-related violence, <a href="/wiki/Marital_rape" title="Marital rape">marital rape</a>, <a href="/wiki/Female_genital_mutilation" title="Female genital mutilation">female genital mutilation</a> and other traditional practices harmful to women, non-spousal violence and violence related to exploitation.</p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_violence">Family violence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Family violence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Family violence is a broader definition, often used to include <a href="/wiki/Child_abuse" title="Child abuse">child abuse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elder_abuse" title="Elder abuse">elder abuse</a>, and other violent acts between family members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wallace_p._2_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wallace_p._2-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>Child abuse is defined by the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">WHO</a> as:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Child maltreatment, sometimes referred to as child abuse and neglect, includes all forms of physical and emotional ill-treatment, sexual abuse, neglect, and exploitation that results in actual or potential harm to the child's health, development or dignity. Within this broad definition, five subtypes can be distinguished – physical abuse; sexual abuse; neglect and negligent treatment; emotional abuse; and exploitation.</p></blockquote>
<p>There exists legislation to prevent and punish the occurrence of these offences. There are laws regarding familial sexual activity, which states that it is a criminal offence to have any kind of sexual relationship between one's grandparent, parent, sibling, aunt or uncle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>Elder abuse is, according to the WHO: "a single, or repeated act, or lack of appropriate action, occurring within any relationship where there is an expectation of trust which causes harm or distress to an older person".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Parental_abuse_of_children_.28child_abuse.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Parental_abuse_of_children_(child_abuse)">Parental abuse of children (child abuse)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Parental abuse of children (child abuse)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parental_abuse_of_children" title="Parental abuse of children">Parental abuse of children</a></div>
<p>Child abuse is the physical, sexual or emotional maltreatment or neglect of a child or children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> In the United States, the <a href="/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">Centers for Disease Control and Prevention</a> (CDC) and the Department for Children and Families (DCF) define child maltreatment as any act or series of acts of commission or omission by a parent or other caregiver that results in harm, potential for harm, or threat of harm to a child.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDC-98">[98]</a></sup> Child abuse can occur in a child's home, or in the organizations, schools or communities the child interacts with. There are four major categories of child abuse: <a href="/wiki/Child_neglect" title="Child neglect">neglect</a>, <a href="/wiki/Physical_abuse" title="Physical abuse">physical abuse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Psychological_abuse" title="Psychological abuse">psychological or emotional abuse</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Child_sexual_abuse" title="Child sexual abuse">sexual abuse</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Parental_abuse_by_children">Parental abuse by children</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Parental abuse by children">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Parental_abuse_by_children" title="Parental abuse by children">Parental abuse by children</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Abuse" title="Abuse">Abuse</a> of parents by their children is a common but under reported and under-researched subject. A factor why this subject is under-researched is because of the overshadowing effect caused by parents abusing their children instead.  Parents are quite often subject to levels of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Childhood_aggression" title="Childhood aggression">childhood aggression</a> in excess of normal childhood aggressive outbursts, typically in the form of <a href="/wiki/Verbal_abuse" title="Verbal abuse">verbal</a> or <a href="/wiki/Physical_abuse" title="Physical abuse">physical</a> abuse. Parents feel a sense of <a href="/wiki/Shame" title="Shame">shame</a> and <a href="/wiki/Humiliation" title="Humiliation">humiliation</a> to have that problem, so they rarely seek help and it is usually little or no help available anyway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Elder_abuse">Elder abuse</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Elder abuse">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Elder_abuse" title="Elder abuse">Elder abuse</a></div>
<p>Elder abuse is "a single, or repeated act, or lack of appropriate action, occurring within any relationship where there is an expectation of trust, which causes harm or distress to an older person".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> This definition has been adopted by the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> from a definition put forward by Action on Elder Abuse in the UK. Laws protecting the elderly from abuse are similar to, and related to, laws protecting <a href="/wiki/Dependent_adult" title="Dependent adult">dependent adults</a> from abuse.
</p><p>The core element to the harm of elder abuse is the "expectation of trust" of the older person toward their abuser. Thus, it includes harms by people the older person knows or with whom they have a relationship, such as a spouse, partner or family member, a friend or neighbor, or people that the older person relies on for services. Many forms of elder abuse are recognized as types of domestic violence or family violence.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Forced_and_child_marriage">Forced and child marriage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Forced and child marriage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Forced_marriage" title="Forced marriage">Forced marriage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Child_marriage" title="Child marriage">Child marriage</a></div>
<p>Forced and child marriages are practiced in certain regions of the world, particularly in <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, and these types of marriages are associated with a high rate of domestic violence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hrw.org_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-hrw.org-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Forced_marriage" title="Forced marriage">forced marriage</a> is a marriage where one or both participants are married without their freely given consent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> The line between forced marriage and consensual marriage may become blurred, because the social norms of many cultures dictate that one should never oppose the desire of one's parents/relatives in regard to the choice of a spouse; in such cultures it is not necessary for violence, threats, intimidation etc. to occur, the person simply "consents" to the marriage even if he/she doesn't want it, out of the implied social pressure and duty. The customs of <a href="/wiki/Bride_price" title="Bride price">bride price</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dowry" title="Dowry">dowry</a>, that exist in parts of the world, can lead to buying and selling people into marriage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>A child marriage is a <a href="/wiki/Marriage" title="Marriage">marriage</a> where one or both spouses are under 18.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unicef.org_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-unicef.org-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hrw.org_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-hrw.org-102">[102]</a></sup> Child marriage was common throughout history but is today condemned by international human rights organizations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc.co.uk_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.co.uk-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who.int_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-who.int-112">[112]</a></sup> Child marriages are often arranged between the families of the future bride and groom, sometimes as soon as the girl is born.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc.co.uk_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.co.uk-110">[110]</a></sup> Child marriages can also occur in the context of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marriage_by_abduction" title="Marriage by abduction">marriage by abduction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc.co.uk_110-2"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.co.uk-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="The_concept_of_family_honour">The concept of family honour</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: The concept of family honour">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Family_honor" title="Family honor">Family honor</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Culture_of_honor" title="Culture of honor">Culture of honor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Honor_killing" title="Honor killing">Honor killing</a></div>
<p>Family honor is an abstract concept involving the perceived quality of worthiness and respectability that affects the social standing and the self-evaluation of a group of related people, both corporately and individually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Malina2001_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Malina2001-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spain_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-spain-114">[114]</a></sup> The family is viewed as the main source of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Honor" title="Honor">honor</a> and the community highly values the relationship between honor and the family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Family_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Family-115">[115]</a></sup> The conduct of family members reflects upon family honor and the way the family perceives itself, and is perceived by others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spain_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-spain-114">[114]</a></sup> In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Culture_of_honor" title="Culture of honor">cultures of honor</a> maintaining the family honor is often perceived as more important than either <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Individual_freedom" title="Individual freedom">individual freedom</a>, or individual achievement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGoldrickGiordano2005_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-McGoldrickGiordano2005-116">[116]</a></sup> In extreme cases, engaging in acts that are deemed to tarnish the honor of the family results in <a href="/wiki/Honor_killing" title="Honor killing">honor killings</a>. An honor killing is the <a href="/wiki/Homicide" title="Homicide">homicide</a> of a member of a family or social group by other members, due to the perpetrators' belief that the victim has brought shame or dishonor upon the family or community, usually for reasons such as refusing to enter an <a href="/wiki/Arranged_marriage" title="Arranged marriage">arranged marriage</a>, being in a relationship that is disapproved by their relatives, having <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sex_outside_marriage" title="Sex outside marriage">sex outside marriage</a>, becoming the victim of <a href="/wiki/Rape" title="Rape">rape</a>, dressing in ways which are deemed inappropriate, or engaging in <a href="/wiki/Homosexuality" title="Homosexuality">homosexual</a> relations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_issues">Economic issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Economic issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>A family is often part of a <a href="/wiki/Sharing_economy" title="Sharing economy">sharing economy</a> with <a href="/wiki/Common_ownership" title="Common ownership">common ownership</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="Dowry.2C_bride_price_and_dower"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Dowry,_bride_price_and_dower">Dowry, bride price and dower</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Dowry, bride price and dower">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Dowry" title="Dowry">Dowry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bride_price" title="Bride price">Bride price</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dower" title="Dower">Dower</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thai_Bride_Price_2008.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2912" data-file-width="4368" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Thai_Bride_Price_2008.jpg/220px-Thai_Bride_Price_2008.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Thai_Bride_Price_2008.jpg/330px-Thai_Bride_Price_2008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Thai_Bride_Price_2008.jpg/440px-Thai_Bride_Price_2008.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thai_Bride_Price_2008.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A traditional, formal presentation of the bride price at a Thai engagement ceremony.</div></div></div>
<p>Dowry is property (money, goods, or estate) that a wife or wife's family gives to her husband when the wife and husband marry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> Offering dowry was common in many cultures historically (including in Europe and North America), but this practice today is mostly restricted to some areas primarily in the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>.
</p><p>Bride price, (also bride wealth or bride token), is property paid by the groom or his family to the parents of a woman upon the marriage of their daughter to the groom. It is practiced mostly in Sub-Saharan Africa, parts of South-East Asia (<a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a>), and parts of Central Asia.
</p><p>Dower is property given to the bride herself by the groom at the time of marriage, and which remains under her ownership and control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Property_regimes_and_taxation">Property regimes and taxation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Property regimes and taxation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In some countries married couples benefit from various taxation advantages not available to a single person or to unmarried couples. For example, spouses may be allowed to average their combined incomes. Some jurisdictions recognize <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Common_law_marriage" title="Common law marriage">common law marriage</a> or <i><a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">de facto</a></i> relations for this purposes. In some jurisdictions there is also an option of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civil_partnership" title="Civil partnership">civil partnership</a> or <a href="/wiki/Domestic_partnership" title="Domestic partnership">domestic partnership</a>.
</p><p>Different property regimes exist for spouses. In many countries, each marriage partner has the choice of keeping their property <a href="/wiki/Community_property" title="Community property">separate</a> or combining properties. In the latter case, called <a href="/wiki/Community_property" title="Community property">community property</a>, when the marriage ends by divorce each owns half. In lieu of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Will_(law)" title="Will (law)">will</a> or <a href="/wiki/Trust_law" title="Trust law">trust</a>, property owned by the deceased generally is inherited by the surviving spouse.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rights_and_laws">Rights and laws</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Rights and laws">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproductive_rights">Reproductive rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Reproductive rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Reproductive_rights" title="Reproductive rights">Reproductive rights</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forced_sterilization" title="Forced sterilization">Forced sterilization</a>, <a href="/wiki/Forced_pregnancy" title="Forced pregnancy">Forced pregnancy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Forced_abortion" title="Forced abortion">Forced abortion</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Total_Fertility_Rate_Map_by_Country.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="566" data-file-width="1104" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Total_Fertility_Rate_Map_by_Country.svg/300px-Total_Fertility_Rate_Map_by_Country.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Total_Fertility_Rate_Map_by_Country.svg/450px-Total_Fertility_Rate_Map_by_Country.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Total_Fertility_Rate_Map_by_Country.svg/600px-Total_Fertility_Rate_Map_by_Country.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Total_Fertility_Rate_Map_by_Country.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of countries by fertility rate (2020), according to the <a href="/wiki/Population_Reference_Bureau" title="Population Reference Bureau">Population Reference Bureau</a></div></div></div>
<p>Reproductive rights are legal rights and freedoms relating to reproduction and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reproductive_health" title="Reproductive health">reproductive health</a>. These include the right to decide on issues regarding the number of children born, family planning, contraception, and private life, free from <a href="/wiki/Coercion" title="Coercion">coercion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Discrimination" title="Discrimination">discrimination</a>; as well as the right to access health services and adequate information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> According to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UNFPA" title="UNFPA">UNFPA</a>, reproductive rights "include the right to decide the number, timing and spacing of children, the right to voluntarily marry and establish a family, and the right to the highest attainable standard of health, among others".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Family_planning" title="Family planning">Family planning</a> refers to the factors that may be considered by individuals and couples in order for them to control their fertility, anticipate and attain the desired number of children and the spacing and timing of their births.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p>The state and church have been, and still are in some countries, involved in controlling the size of families, often using coercive methods, such as bans on contraception or abortion (where the policy is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natalist" title="Natalist">natalist</a> one—for example through <a href="/wiki/Tax_on_childlessness" title="Tax on childlessness">tax on childlessness</a>) or conversely, discriminatory policies against large families or even <a href="/wiki/Forced_abortion" title="Forced abortion">forced abortions</a> (e.g., China's <a href="/wiki/One-child_policy" title="One-child policy">one-child policy</a> in place from 1978 to 2015). <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forced_sterilization" title="Forced sterilization">Forced sterilization</a> has often targeted ethnic minority groups, such as Roma women in Eastern Europe,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> or indigenous women in Peru (during the 1990s).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Parents.27_rights"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Parents'_rights">Parents' rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Parents' rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Parents%27_rights_movement" title="Parents' rights movement">parents' rights</a> movement is a movement whose members are primarily interested in issues affecting parents and children related to family law, specifically parental rights and obligations. <a href="/wiki/Mothers%27_rights" title="Mothers' rights">Mothers' rights</a> movements focus on <a href="/wiki/Maternal_health" title="Maternal health">maternal health</a>, workplace issues such as <a href="/wiki/Labor_rights" title="Labor rights">labor rights</a>, <a href="/wiki/Breastfeeding" title="Breastfeeding">breastfeeding</a>, and rights in <a href="/wiki/Family_law" title="Family law">family law</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fathers%27_rights" title="Fathers' rights">fathers' rights</a> movement is a movement whose members are primarily interested in issues related to <a href="/wiki/Family_law" title="Family law">family law</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Child_custody" title="Child custody">child custody</a> and <a href="/wiki/Child_support" title="Child support">child support</a>, that affect fathers and their <a href="/wiki/Child" title="Child">children</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collier2006_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collier2006-134">[134]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Children.27s_rights"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Children's_rights">Children's rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Children's rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Children%27s_rights" title="Children's rights">Children's rights</a></div>
<p>Children's rights are the human rights of children, with particular attention to the rights of special protection and care afforded to minors, including their right to association with both parents, their right to human identity, their right to be provided in regard to their other basic needs, and their right to be free from violence and abuse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriage_rights">Marriage rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Marriage rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marriage_laws" title="Marriage laws">Marriage laws</a></div>
<p>Each jurisdiction has its own <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marriage_laws" title="Marriage laws">marriage laws</a>. These laws differ significantly from country to country; and these laws are often controversial. Areas of controversy include <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_rights" title="Women's rights">women's rights</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage" title="Same-sex marriage">same-sex marriage</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_reforms">Legal reforms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Legal reforms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Legal reforms to <a href="/wiki/Family_law" title="Family law">family laws</a> have taken place in many countries during the past few decades. These dealt primarily with <a href="/wiki/Gender_equality" title="Gender equality">gender equality</a> within marriage and with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Divorce_law" title="Divorce law">divorce laws</a>. Women have been given equal rights in marriage in many countries, reversing older family laws based on the dominant legal role of the husband. <a href="/wiki/Coverture" title="Coverture">Coverture</a>, which was enshrined in the <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a> of England and the US for several centuries and throughout most of the 19th century, was abolished. In some European countries the changes that lead to gender equality were slower. The period of 1975–1979 saw a major overhaul of <a href="/wiki/Family_law" title="Family law">family laws</a> in countries such as Italy,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> Spain,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> Austria,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Contemporary_Western_European_Feminism_pp._133_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Contemporary_Western_European_Feminism_pp._133-141">[141]</a></sup> West Germany,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> and Portugal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> In 1978, the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Europe" title="Council of Europe">Council of Europe</a> passed the <i>Resolution (78) 37 on equality of spouses in civil law</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> Among the last European countries to establish full <a href="/wiki/Gender_equality" title="Gender equality">gender equality</a> in marriage were Switzerland. In 1985, a referendum guaranteed women legal equality with men within marriage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> The new reforms came into force in January 1988.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> In Greece, in 1983, legislation was passed guaranteeing equality between spouses, abolishing <a href="/wiki/Dowry" title="Dowry">dowry</a>, and ending legal discrimination against illegitimate children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> In 1981, Spain abolished the requirement that married women must have their husbands' permission to initiate judicial proceedings<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wbl.worldbank.org_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-wbl.worldbank.org-151">[151]</a></sup> the Netherlands,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> and France<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[note 1]</a></sup> in the 1980s. In recent decades, the <a href="/wiki/Marital_power" title="Marital power">marital power</a> has also been abolished in African countries that had this doctrine, but many African countries that were former <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_colonies" title="French colonies">French colonies</a> still have discriminatory laws in their marriages regulations, such regulations originating in the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Code" title="Napoleonic Code">Napoleonic Code</a> that has inspired these laws.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wbl.worldbank.org_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-wbl.worldbank.org-151">[151]</a></sup> In some countries (predominantly Roman Catholic) divorce was legalized only recently (e.g. Italy (1970), Portugal (1975), Brazil (1977), Spain (1981), Argentina (1987), Ireland (1996), Chile (2004) and Malta (2011)) although <a href="/wiki/Annulment" title="Annulment">annulment</a> and <a href="/wiki/Legal_separation" title="Legal separation">legal separation</a> were options. Philippines still does not allow divorce. (see <a href="/wiki/Divorce_law_by_country" title="Divorce law by country">Divorce law by country</a>). The laws pertaining to the situation of children born outside marriage have also been revised in many countries (see <a href="/wiki/Legitimacy_(family_law)" title="Legitimacy (family law)">Legitimacy (family law)</a>).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maternal_mortality_rate_worldwide.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="820" data-file-width="1800" decoding="async" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Maternal_mortality_rate_worldwide.jpg/300px-Maternal_mortality_rate_worldwide.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Maternal_mortality_rate_worldwide.jpg/450px-Maternal_mortality_rate_worldwide.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Maternal_mortality_rate_worldwide.jpg/600px-Maternal_mortality_rate_worldwide.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maternal_mortality_rate_worldwide.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Global maternal mortality rate per 100 000 live births, (2010)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIA_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-157">[156]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_medicine">Family medicine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Family medicine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Family_medicine" title="Family medicine">Family medicine</a></div>
<p>Family medicine is a medical specialty devoted to comprehensive health care for people of all ages; it is based on knowledge of the patient in the context of the family and the community, emphasizing disease prevention and health promotion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[157]</a></sup> The importance of family medicine is being increasingly recognized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[158]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2012_Infant_mortality_rate_per_1000_live_births,_under-5,_world_map.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="443" data-file-width="863" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/2012_Infant_mortality_rate_per_1000_live_births%2C_under-5%2C_world_map.svg/300px-2012_Infant_mortality_rate_per_1000_live_births%2C_under-5%2C_world_map.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/2012_Infant_mortality_rate_per_1000_live_births%2C_under-5%2C_world_map.svg/450px-2012_Infant_mortality_rate_per_1000_live_births%2C_under-5%2C_world_map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/2012_Infant_mortality_rate_per_1000_live_births%2C_under-5%2C_world_map.svg/600px-2012_Infant_mortality_rate_per_1000_live_births%2C_under-5%2C_world_map.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2012_Infant_mortality_rate_per_1000_live_births,_under-5,_world_map.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>World infant mortality rates in 2012<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[159]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Maternal_mortality">Maternal mortality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Maternal mortality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maternal_mortality" title="Maternal mortality">Maternal mortality</a></div>
<p>Maternal mortality or maternal death is defined by <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">WHO</a> as "the death of a woman while pregnant or within 42 days of termination of pregnancy, irrespective of the duration and site of the pregnancy, from any cause related to or aggravated by the pregnancy or its management but not from accidental or incidental causes."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-161">[160]</a></sup> Historically, maternal mortality was a major cause of women's death. In recent decades, advances in healthcare have resulted in rates of maternal mortality having dropped dramatically, especially in Western countries. Maternal mortality however remains a serious problem in many African and Asian counties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_161-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-161">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[161]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Infant_and_child_mortality">Infant and child mortality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Infant and child mortality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Infant_mortality" title="Infant mortality">Infant mortality</a> and <a href="/wiki/Child_mortality" title="Child mortality">Child mortality</a></div>
<p>Infant mortality is the death of a child less than one year of age. Child mortality is the death of a child before the child's fifth birthday. Like maternal mortality, infant and child mortality were common throughout history, but have decreased significantly in modern times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[162]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[163]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Parents_with_child_Statue_Hrobakova_street_Bratislava.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" decoding="async" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Parents_with_child_Statue_Hrobakova_street_Bratislava.JPG/250px-Parents_with_child_Statue_Hrobakova_street_Bratislava.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Parents_with_child_Statue_Hrobakova_street_Bratislava.JPG/375px-Parents_with_child_Statue_Hrobakova_street_Bratislava.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Parents_with_child_Statue_Hrobakova_street_Bratislava.JPG/500px-Parents_with_child_Statue_Hrobakova_street_Bratislava.JPG 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Parents_with_child_Statue_Hrobakova_street_Bratislava.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Parents with child statue, Hrobákova street, Petržalka, <a href="/wiki/Bratislava" title="Bratislava">Bratislava</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Family_sculpture_by_Robert_Thomas,_Cardiff_Queen_Street_-_geograph.org.uk_-_624471.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="375" decoding="async" height="333" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/The_Family_sculpture_by_Robert_Thomas%2C_Cardiff_Queen_Street_-_geograph.org.uk_-_624471.jpg/250px-The_Family_sculpture_by_Robert_Thomas%2C_Cardiff_Queen_Street_-_geograph.org.uk_-_624471.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bd/The_Family_sculpture_by_Robert_Thomas%2C_Cardiff_Queen_Street_-_geograph.org.uk_-_624471.jpg 1.5x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Family_sculpture_by_Robert_Thomas,_Cardiff_Queen_Street_-_geograph.org.uk_-_624471.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Family</i>, a sculpture by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Thomas_(sculptor)" title="Robert Thomas (sculptor)">Robert Thomas</a>, in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardiff,_Wales" title="Cardiff, Wales">Cardiff, Wales</a></div></div></div>
<p>While in many parts of the world family policies seek to promote a gender-equal organization of the family life, in others the male-dominated family continues to be the official policy of the authorities, which is also supported by law. For instance, the Civil Code of <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> states at Article 1105: "In relations between husband and wife; the position of the head of the family is the exclusive right of the husband".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[164]</a></sup>
</p><p>In some parts of the world, some governments promote a specific form of family, such as that based on traditional <a href="/wiki/Family_values" title="Family values">family values</a>. The term "family values" is often used in political discourse in some countries, its general meaning being that of traditional or cultural values that pertain to the family's structure, function, roles, beliefs, attitudes, and ideals, usually involving the "traditional family"—a <a href="/wiki/Middle_class" title="Middle class">middle-class</a> family with a breadwinner father and a homemaker mother, raising their biological children. Any deviation from this family model is considered a "nontraditional family".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[165]</a></sup> These family ideals are often advanced through policies such as <a href="/wiki/Marriage_promotion" title="Marriage promotion">marriage promotion</a>. Some jurisdictions outlaw practices which they deem as socially or religiously unacceptable, such as <a href="/wiki/Fornication" title="Fornication">fornication</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cohabitation" title="Cohabitation">cohabitation</a> or <a href="/wiki/Adultery" title="Adultery">adultery</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Work-family_balance">Work-family balance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Work-family balance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Work%E2%80%93family_balance_in_the_United_States" title="Work–family balance in the United States">Work–family balance in the United States</a></div>
<p>Work-family balance is a concept involving proper prioritizing between work/career and family life. It includes issues relating to the way how work and families intersect and influence each other. At a political level, it is reflected through policies such <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maternity_leave" title="Maternity leave">maternity leave</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paternity_leave" title="Paternity leave">paternity leave</a>. Since the 1950s, social scientists as well as feminists have increasingly criticized gendered arrangements of work and care, and the male breadwinner role, and policies are increasingly targeting men as fathers, as a tool of changing gender relations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[166]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Protection_of_private_and_family_life">Protection of private and family life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Protection of private and family life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Article 8 of the <a href="/wiki/European_Convention_on_Human_Rights" title="European Convention on Human Rights">European Convention on Human Rights</a> provides a right to respect for one's "private and family life, his home and his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Privacy_of_correspondence" title="Privacy of correspondence">correspondence</a>", subject to certain restrictions that are "in accordance with law" and "<a href="/wiki/Necessary_in_a_democratic_society" title="Necessary in a democratic society">necessary in a democratic society</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[167]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p><b>Article 8 – Right to respect for private and family life</b>
</p><p>1. Everyone has the right to respect for his private and family life, his home and his correspondence.
</p><p>
2. There shall be no interference by a public authority with the exercise of this right except such as is in accordance with the law and is necessary in a democratic society in the interests of national security, public safety or the economic well-being of the country, for the prevention of disorder or crime, for the protection of health or morals, or for the protection of the rights and freedom of others.</p></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">Criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Criticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Certain social scientists<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (July 2022)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> have advocated the abolition of the family<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Vagueness" title="Wikipedia:Vagueness"><span title="This information is too vague. (July 2022)">vague</span></a></i>]</sup>. An early opponent of the family was Socrates whose position was outlined by Plato in <i>The Republic</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[168]</a></sup> In Book 5 of <i>The Republic</i>, Socrates tells his interlocutors that a just city is one in which citizens have no family ties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[170]</a></sup>
</p><p>The family being such a deep-rooted and much-venerated institution, few intellectuals have ventured to speak against it. <a href="/wiki/Familialism" title="Familialism">Familialism</a> has been atypically defined as a "social structure where ... a family's values are held in higher esteem than the values of the individual members of the family". <a href="/wiki/In-group_favoritism" title="In-group favoritism">Favoritism</a> granted to relatives regardless of <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/merit" title="wikt:merit">merit</a> is called <a href="/wiki/Nepotism" title="Nepotism">nepotism</a>.
</p><p>The Russian-American <a href="/wiki/Rationalism" title="Rationalism">rationalist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Individualism" title="Individualism">individualist</a> philosopher, novelist and playwright <a href="/wiki/Ayn_Rand" title="Ayn Rand">Ayn Rand</a> compared partiality towards <a href="/wiki/Consanguinity" title="Consanguinity">consanguinity</a> with <a href="/wiki/Racism" title="Racism">racism</a>, as a small-scale manifestation of the latter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rand_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-rand-172">[171]</a></sup> "The worship of the family is merely racism, like a crudely primitive first installment on the worship of the tribe. It places the <a href="/wiki/Accident_of_birth" title="Accident of birth">accident of birth</a> above a man's values and duty to the tribe above a man's right to his own life."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[172]</a></sup> Additionally, she spoke in favor of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Childfree" title="Childfree">childfree</a> lifestyle, while following it herself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rand_172-1"><a href="#cite_note-rand-172">[171]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="The_family_and_social_justice">The family and social justice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: The family and social justice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>One of the controversies regarding the family is the application of the concept of <a href="/wiki/Social_justice" title="Social justice">social justice</a> to the private sphere of family relations, in particular with regard to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rights_of_women" title="Rights of women">rights of women</a> and <a href="/wiki/Children%27s_rights" title="Children's rights">children</a>. Throughout much of the history, most philosophers who advocated for social justice focused on the public political arena, not on the family structures; with the family often being seen as a separate entity which needed to be protected from outside state intrusion. One notable exception was <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">John Stuart Mill</a>, who, in his work <i><a href="/wiki/The_Subjection_of_Women" title="The Subjection of Women">The Subjection of Women</a></i>, advocated for greater rights for women within marriage and family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[173]</a></sup> Second wave feminists argued that <a href="/wiki/The_personal_is_political" title="The personal is political">the personal is political</a>, stating that there are strong connections between personal experiences and the larger social and political structures. In the context of the feminist movement of the 1960s and 1970s, this was a challenge to the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_family" title="Nuclear family">nuclear family</a> and <a href="/wiki/Family_values" title="Family values">family values</a>, as they were understood then.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harutyunyan2009p50_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harutyunyan2009p50-175">[174]</a></sup> Feminists focused on <a href="/wiki/Domestic_violence" title="Domestic violence">domestic violence</a>, arguing that the reluctance—in law or in practice—of the state to intervene and offer protection to women who have been abused within the family, is in violation of women's <a href="/wiki/Human_rights" title="Human rights">human rights</a>, and is the result of an ideology which places family relations outside the conceptual framework of human rights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[175]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_trends_in_family_composition">Global trends in family composition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Global trends in family composition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Statistics from an infographic by Olivier Ballou showed that,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AEI_2015_Eberstadt_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-AEI_2015_Eberstadt-177">[176]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>In 2013, just over 40% of US babies were born outside marriage. The Census bureau estimated that 27% of all children lived in a fatherless home. Europe has seen a surge in child-free adults. One in five 40-something women are childless in Sweden and in Switzerland, in Italy one in four, in Berlin one in three. So-called traditional societies are seeing the same trend. About one-sixth of Japanese women in their forties have never married and about 30% of all women that age are childless.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite><a class="external text" href="https://www.aei.org/multimedia/global-flight-family" rel="nofollow">Infographic Olivier Ballou (AEI)</a></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>However, Swedish statisticians reported in 2013 that, in contrast to many countries, since the 2000s, fewer children have experienced their parents' separation, childlessness had decreased in Sweden and marriages had increased. It had also become more common for couples to have a third child suggesting that the nuclear family was no longer in decline in Sweden.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unece_2013_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-unece_2013-178">[177]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 10">: 10 </span></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-156"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-156">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Although married women in France obtained the right to work without their husbands' permission in 1965,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> and the paternal authority of a man over his family was ended in 1970 (before that parental responsibilities belonged solely to the father who made all legal decisions concerning the children), it was only in 1985 that a legal reform abolished the stipulation that the husband had the sole power to administer the children's property.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li id="cite_note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-12">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"We have seen that people can refer to their relatives as 'the family.' 'All the family turned up for the funeral... But of course, my brother didn't bring his family along— they're much too young.' Here the reference is to the offspring (as distinct from 'all' the family). The neighbors were very good, too. 'The Jones came, and their two children. It was nice, the whole family turning up like that.' Here the usage is more restricted than 'relatives' or 'his relatives,' but includes just both parents and offspring. 'Of course, the children will be leaving home soon. It's always sad to see the family break up like that.' Here the reference is not only to parents and children but to their co-residence, that is, to the household."The Family and Industrial Society", 1983, George Allen Unwin, London, p. 30</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-29">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Sanford L. Braver and Michael E. Lamb, <a class="external text" href="https://www.researchgate.net/profile/Sanford_Braver/publication/324422710_Shared_Parenting_After_Parental_Separation_The_Views_of_12_Experts/links/5ad8c563aca272fdaf81f6fb/Shared-Parenting-After-Parental-Separation-The-Views-of-12-Experts.pdf" rel="nofollow">Shared Parenting After Parental Separation: The Views of 12 Experts</a>, <i>Journal of Divorce &amp; Remarriage</i>, April 2018: "The empirical evidence currently available supports the view that children of divorce, on average, benefit substantially from SP [shared parenting] arrangements in which they live with each parent at least 35% of the time. Findings from well over 50 individual studies indicate that children whose parents have SP fare better than those with sole physical custody [...]."</span>

<li id="cite_note-59"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-59">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">By replacement in the definition of the notion of "generation" by <a href="/wiki/Meiosis" title="Meiosis">meiosis</a>". Since identical twins are not separated by meiosis, there are no "generations" between them, hence <i>n</i>=0 and <i>r</i>=1. See <a class="external text" href="http://www.genetic-genealogy.co.uk/Toc115570135.html" rel="nofollow">genetic-genealogy.co.uk</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210224210418/http://www.genetic-genealogy.co.uk/Toc115570135.html" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 2021-02-24 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>.</span>
